<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Hindustani language</span></h1>

<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Indo-Aryan language spoken in India and Pakistan</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For its official forms, see <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Hindi" title="Fiji Hindi">Fijian Hindustani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">Caribbean Hindustani</a>.</div>

– Hindi<br/><code><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/php/langcodes_name.php?iso_639_1=ur" rel="nofollow">ur</a></span></code> – Urdu</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-2" title="ISO 639-2">ISO 639-2</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/php/langcodes_name.php?code_ID=188" rel="nofollow">hin</a></span></code> – Hindi<br/><code><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/php/langcodes_name.php?code_ID=475" rel="nofollow">urd</a></span></code> – Urdu</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">Either:<br/><code><a class="extiw" href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/hin" title="iso639-3:hin">hin</a></code> – Hindi<br/><code><a class="extiw" href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/urd" title="iso639-3:urd">urd</a></code> – Urdu</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></i></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/hind1270" rel="nofollow">hind1270</a></code></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code>59-AAF-qa to -qf</code></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hindustani_map.png"><img alt="Hindustani map.png" data-file-height="814" data-file-width="877" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Hindustani_map.png/220px-Hindustani_map.png" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kauravi" title="Kauravi">Kauravi</a>) is the native language</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)"><img alt="&lt;?&gt;" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Replacement_character.svg/40px-Replacement_character.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Replacement_character.svg/60px-Replacement_character.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Replacement_character.svg/80px-Replacement_character.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">You may need <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support#Kaithi" title="Help:Multilingual support">rendering support</a> to display the uncommon <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters in this article correctly.</div></div>
</div>
<p><b>Hindustani</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>: <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span lang="inc-Deva">हिन्दुस्तानी</span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hindustani-devn-spelling_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-hindustani-devn-spelling-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup> <span title="ISO 15919 Indic (Indic languages language) transliteration"><i lang="inc-Latn">Hindustānī</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" title="Nastaliq">Perso-Arabic</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[c]</a></sup> <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">ہندوستانی</span></span></span>, <span title="ISO 15919 Indic (Indic languages language) transliteration"><i lang="inc-Latn">Hindūstānī</i></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'of <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a>')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCSU-Hindustani-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ray2011_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ray2011-4">[3]</a></sup> is the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">Northern</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Central</a> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-siddiqi1994-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-2018-hindustani-language_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-2018-hindustani-language-15">[12]</a></sup> Hindustani is a  <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric language</a> with two <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard</a> <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>. Thus, the language is sometimes called <b>Hindi–Urdu.</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trask-hclinguistics_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-trask-hclinguistics-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcrystal-dict-lang_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-dcrystal-dict-lang-17">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gandhi-hindi-urdu_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gandhi-hindi-urdu-18">[15]</a></sup> Despite these standard registers, colloquial speech in Hindustani often exists on a spectrum between these standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basu2017_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basu2017-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-GubeGao2019-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancestors of the language were known as: <i>Hindui</i>, <i>Hindavi</i>, <i>Zabān-e <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hind</a></i> (<abbr title="translation">transl.</abbr><span> 'Language of India'</span>), <i>Zabān-e <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a></i> (<abbr title="translation">transl.</abbr><span> 'Language of Hindustan'</span>), <i>Hindustan ki boli</i> (<abbr title="translation">transl.</abbr><span> 'Language of Hindustan'</span>), <a href="/wiki/Rekhta" title="Rekhta">Rekhta</a>, and Hindi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-siddiqi1994-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pulsipher2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pulsipher2005-21">[18]</a></sup> Its regional dialects became known as <i>Zabān-e Dakhani</i> in southern India, <i>Zabān-e Gujari</i> (<abbr title="translation">transl.</abbr><span> 'Language of Gujars'</span>) in Gujarat, and as <i>Zabān-e Dehlavi</i> or Urdu around Delhi. It is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan language</a>, deriving its base primarily from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Indo-Aryan_languages#Languages" title="Central Indo-Aryan languages">Western Hindi</a> dialect of <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Old_Hindi" title="Old Hindi">Khariboli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier2010_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier2010-22">[19]</a></sup> Hindustani is a <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric language</a>, best characterised as a continuum between two <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standardised</a> <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Modern Standard Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Modern Standard Urdu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basu2017_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Basu2017-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-GubeGao2019-20">[17]</a></sup> Depending on the social context and geographical area, the language leans towards either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of a Hindustani language as a "unifying language" or "fusion language" was endorsed by Mahatma Gandhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> The conversion from Hindi to Urdu (or vice versa) is generally achieved just by <a href="/wiki/Hindi%E2%80%93Urdu_transliteration" title="Hindi–Urdu transliteration">transliteration between the two scripts</a>, instead of translation which is generally only required for religious and literary texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars trace the language's first written poetry, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Old_Hindi" title="Old Hindi">Old Hindi</a>, to as early as 769 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup> However this view is not generally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> During the period of the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, which covered most of today's India, eastern Pakistan, southern Nepal and Bangladesh<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> and which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Ganga-Jamuni_tehzeeb" title="Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb">contact of Hindu and Muslim cultures</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> base of Old Hindi became enriched with loanwords from <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, evolving into the present form of Hindustani.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rekhta2020_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rekhta2020-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MatthewsShackleHusain1985_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-MatthewsShackleHusain1985-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhulipala2000_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dhulipala2000-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJSW1943_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJSW1943-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mody2008_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mody2008-35">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kesavan1997_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kesavan1997-36">[33]</a></sup> The Hindustani vernacular became an expression of Indian national unity during the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian Independence movement</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hock1991_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hock1991-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-38">[35]</a></sup> and continues to be spoken as the common language of the people of the northern Indian subcontinent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ashmore1961-39">[36]</a></sup> which is reflected in the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_vocabulary" title="Hindustani vocabulary">Hindustani vocabulary</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> films and songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tunstall2008_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tunstall2008-40">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiro2015_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiro2015-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The language's core vocabulary is derived from <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> (a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-GubeGao2019-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper2010-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997-43">[40]</a></sup> with substantial <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_etymology" title="Hindustani etymology">loanwords</a> from Persian and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (via Persian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper2003_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Draper2003-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmad2002_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmad2002-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalmia2017_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalmia2017-46">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">Hindustani</a> also borrowed Persian prefixes to create new words. <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> affixes became so assimilated that they were used with original <a href="/wiki/Kauravi_dialect" title="Kauravi dialect">Khari Boli</a> words as well. The process of hybridization also led to the formation of words in which the first element of the compound was from Khari Boli and the second from <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, such as <i>rajmahal</i> ‘palace’ (<i>raja</i> ‘royal, king’ + <i>mahal</i> ‘house, place’) and <i>rangmahal</i> ‘fashion house’ (<i>rang</i> ‘colour, dye’ + <i>mahal</i> ‘house, place’).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Islamic_rulers_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Islamic rulers in the Indian subcontinent">Muslim rule</a> expanded, Hindustani speakers traveled to distant parts of India as administrators, soldiers, merchants, and artisans. As it reached new areas, Hindustani further hybridized with local languages. In the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a>, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">Hindustani</a> blended with <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a> and came to be called <a href="/wiki/Deccani_language" title="Deccani language">Dakhani</a>. In Dakhani, aspirated consonants were replaced with their unaspirated counterparts; for instance, <i>dekh</i> ‘see’ became <i>dek</i>, <i>ghula</i> ‘dissolved’ became <i>gula</i>, <i>kuch</i> ‘some’ became <i>kuc</i>, and <i>samajh</i> ‘understand’ became <i>samaj</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of speakers can only be estimated. <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> reports that, as of 2020, <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> together constitute the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_total_number_of_speakers" title="List of languages by total number of speakers">3rd-most-spoken language in the world</a> after English and Mandarin, with 810 million native and second-language speakers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> though this includes millions who self-reported their language as 'Hindi' on the Indian census but speak a number of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_languages" title="Hindi languages">Hindi languages</a> than Hindustani.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> The total number of Hindi–Urdu speakers was reported to be over 300 million in 1995, making Hindustani the third- or fourth-most spoken language in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gambhir1995_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gambhir1995-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p>

</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Hindustani_and_Bollywood"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Hindustani and Bollywood</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hindustani" title="History of Hindustani">History of Hindustani</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Persian_language_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Persian language in the Indian subcontinent">Persian language in the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>Early forms of present-day Hindustani developed from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">Middle Indo-Aryan</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Apabhra%E1%B9%83%C5%9Ba" title="Apabhraṃśa">apabhraṃśa</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernaculars</a> of present-day <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a> in the 7th–13th centuries, chiefly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehlavi_dialect" title="Dehlavi dialect">Dehlavi</a> dialect of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Hindi" title="Western Hindi">Western Hindi</a> category of Indo-Aryan languages that is known as <a href="/wiki/Old_Hindi" title="Old Hindi">Old Hindi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brill1993_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brill1993-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mody2008_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mody2008-35">[32]</a></sup> Hindustani emerged as a contact language around <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>, a result of the increasing linguistic diversity that occurred due to Muslim rule, while the use of its southern dialect, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deccani_Language" title="Deccani Language">Dakhani</a>, was promoted by Muslim rulers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deccan" title="Deccan">Deccan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prakāśaṃ_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prakāśaṃ-54">[51]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrow" title="Amir Khusrow">Amir Khusrow</a>, who lived in the thirteenth century during the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> period in North India, used these forms (which was the <i>lingua franca</i> of the period) in his writings and referred to it as <i>Hindavi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ھندوی</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'of <i>Hind</i> or <i>India</i>').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown2008_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown2008-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kesavan1997_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kesavan1997-36">[33]</a></sup> The Delhi Sultanate, which comprised several <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Afghanistan" title="Demographics of Afghanistan">Afghan</a> dynasties that ruled much of the subcontinent from Delhi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gat_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gat-56">[53]</a></sup> was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> in 1526.
</p><p>Although the Mughals were of <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> (<i>Gurkānī</i>) <a href="/wiki/Turco-Mongol_tradition" title="Turco-Mongol tradition">Turco-Mongol</a> descent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thackston_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thackston-57">[54]</a></sup> they were <a href="/wiki/Persianization" title="Persianization">Persianised</a>, and Persian had gradually become the state language of the Mughal empire after <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-EI-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> a continuation since the introduction of Persian by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Asian" title="Central Asian">Central Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> rulers in the Indian Subcontinent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> and the patronisation of it by the earlier Turko-Afghan Delhi Sultanate. The basis in general for the introduction of Persian into the subcontinent was set, from its earliest days, by various Persianised Central Asian Turkic and Afghan dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindustani began to take shape as a Persianised vernacular during the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> (1206–1526 AD) and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> (1526–1858 AD) in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taj_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taj-64">[61]</a></sup> Hindustani retained the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_vocabulary" title="Hindustani vocabulary">core vocabulary</a> of the local Delhi dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taj_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taj-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strnad2013_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strnad2013-65">[62]</a></sup> However, as an emerging common dialect, Hindustani absorbed large numbers of Persian, Arabic, and Turkic loanwords, and as Mughal conquests grew it spread as a lingua franca across much of northern India; this was a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu-Muslim_unity" title="Hindu-Muslim unity">contact of Hindu and Muslim cultures</a> in Hindustan that created a composite <a href="/wiki/Ganga-Jamuni_tehzeeb" title="Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb">Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhulipala2000_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dhulipala2000-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rekhta2020_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rekhta2020-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJSW1943_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-IJSW1943-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farooqi2012_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farooqi2012-66">[63]</a></sup> The language was also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Rekhta" title="Rekhta">Rekhta</a></i>, or 'mixed', which implies that it was mixed with Persian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayres2009_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ayres2009-68">[65]</a></sup> Written in the <a href="/wiki/Urdu_alphabet" title="Urdu alphabet">Perso-Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgregor_912_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgregor_912-69">[66]</a></sup> and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Kaithi" title="Kaithi">Kaithi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> scripts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> it remained the primary lingua franca of northern India for the next four centuries, although it varied significantly in vocabulary depending on the local language. Alongside Persian, it achieved the status of a literary language in Muslim courts and was also used for literary purposes in various other settings such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sant_(religion)" title="Sant (religion)">Nirgun Sant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Krishna Bhakta</a> circles, and <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> Hindu courts. Its majors centres of development included the Mughal courts of Delhi, <a href="/wiki/Lucknow" title="Lucknow">Lucknow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> as well as the Rajput courts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Amber" title="Kingdom of Amber">Amber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaipur" title="Jaipur">Jaipur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 18th century, towards the end of the Mughal period, with the fragmentation of the empire and the elite system, a variant of Hindustani, one of the successors of <a href="/wiki/Apabhra%E1%B9%83%C5%9Ba" title="Apabhraṃśa">apabhraṃśa</a> vernaculars at Delhi, and nearby cities, came to gradually replace Persian as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> among the educated elite <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">upper class</a> particularly in northern India, though Persian still retained much of its pre-eminence for a short period. The term <i>Hindustani</i> was given to that language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> The Perso-Arabic script form of this language underwent a standardisation process and further Persianisation during this period (18th century) and came to be known as Urdu, a name derived from Persian: <i>Zabān-e Urdū-e Mualla</i> ('language of the court') or <i>Zabān-e Urdū</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">زبان اردو</span></span>, 'language of the camp'). The etymology of the word <i>Urdu</i> is of <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Chagatai</a> origin, <i>Ordū</i> ('camp'), cognate with English <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/horde" title="wikt:horde">horde</a></i>, and known in local translation as <i>Lashkari Zabān</i> (<span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">لشکری زبان</span></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> which is shorted to <i>Lashkari</i> (<span title="Urdu-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ur"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">لشکری</span></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmad2009_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmad2009-74">[71]</a></sup> This is all due to its origin as the common speech of the Mughal army. As a literary language, Urdu took shape in courtly, elite settings. Along with English, it became the first official language of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> in 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coatsworth2015_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coatsworth2015-75">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindi as a standardised literary <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delhi_dialect" title="Delhi dialect">Delhi dialect</a> arose in the 19th century; the <a href="/wiki/Braj_Bhasha" title="Braj Bhasha">Braj dialect</a> was the dominant literary language in the Devanagari script up until and through the 19th century. While the first literary works (mostly translations of earlier works) in Sanskritised Hindustani were already written in the early 19th century as part of a literary project that included both Hindu and Muslim writers (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lallu_Lal" title="Lallu Lal">Lallu Lal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insha_Allah_Khan" title="Insha Allah Khan">Insha Allah Khan</a>), the call for a distinct Sanskritised standard of the Delhi dialect written in Devanagari under the name of Hindi became increasingly politicised in the course of the century and gained pace around 1880 in an effort to displace Urdu's official position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Fletcher_Hurst" title="John Fletcher Hurst">John Fletcher Hurst</a> in his book published in 1891 mentioned that the Hindustani or camp language of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>'s courts at Delhi was not regarded by philologists as a distinct language but only as a dialect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Zone_(Hindi)" title="Central Zone (Hindi)">Hindi</a> with admixture of Persian. He continued: "But it has all the magnitude and importance of separate language. It is linguistic result of Muslim rule of eleventh &amp; twelfth centuries and is spoken (except in rural <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>) by many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a> and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Musalman</a> population in all parts of India." Next to English it was the official language of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, was commonly written in Arabic or Persian characters, and was spoken by approximately 100,000,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the British colonised the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> from the late 18th through to the late 19th century, they used the words 'Hindustani', 'Hindi', and 'Urdu' interchangeably. They developed it as the language of administration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-79">[76]</a></sup> further preparing it to be the official language of modern India and Pakistan. However, with independence, use of the word 'Hindustani' declined, being largely replaced by 'Hindi' and 'Urdu', or 'Hindi-Urdu' when either of those was too specific. More recently, the word 'Hindustani' has been used for the colloquial language of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> films, which are popular in both India and Pakistan and which cannot be unambiguously identified as either Hindi or Urdu.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Registers">Registers</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hindi%E2%80%93Urdu_controversy" title="Hindi–Urdu controversy">Hindi–Urdu controversy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">Register (sociolinguistics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Digraphia" title="Digraphia">digraphia</a></div>
<p>Although, at the spoken level, Hindi and Urdu are considered <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a> of a single language, Hindustani or Hindi-Urdu, as they share a common <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">grammar</a> and core vocabulary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basu2017_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Basu2017-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-GubeGao2019-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeterDass2019_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-PeterDass2019-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper2010-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup> they differ in literary and formal vocabulary; where literary Hindi draws heavily on Sanskrit and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a>, literary Urdu draws heavily on Persian and Arabic loanwords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JainCardona2007_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-JainCardona2007-81">[78]</a></sup> The grammar and base vocabulary (most pronouns, verbs, adpositions, etc.) of both Hindi and Urdu, however, are the same and derive from a Prakritic base, and both have Persian/Arabic influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeterDass2019_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-PeterDass2019-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:572px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="906" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg/570px-Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg/855px-Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg 2x" width="570"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cropped_Image_20220925_180836.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Testament cover page in Hindustani language was published in 1842</div></div></div>
<p>The standardised registers Hindi and Urdu are collectively known as <i>Hindi-Urdu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCSU-Hindustani-13">[10]</a></sup> Hindustani is perhaps the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the north and west of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, though it is understood fairly well in other regions also, especially in the urban areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-siddiqi1994-14">[11]</a></sup> This has led it to be characterised as a continuum that ranges between Hindi and Urdu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[20]</a></sup> A common vernacular sharing characteristics with Sanskritised Hindi, regional Hindi and Urdu, Hindustani is more commonly used as a vernacular than highly Sanskritised Hindi or highly Persianised Urdu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ashmore1961-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>This can be seen in the popular culture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> or, more generally, the vernacular of North Indians and Pakistanis, which generally employs a lexicon common to both <i>Hindi</i> and <i>Urdu</i> speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiro2015_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hiro2015-41">[38]</a></sup> Minor subtleties in region will also affect the 'brand' of Hindustani, sometimes pushing the Hindustani closer to Urdu or to Hindi. One might reasonably assume that the Hindustani spoken in <a href="/wiki/Lucknow" title="Lucknow">Lucknow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> (known for its usage of Urdu) and <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a> (a holy city for Hindus and thus using highly Sanskritised Hindi) is somewhat different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCSU-Hindustani-13">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Standard_Hindi">Modern Standard Hindi</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a></div>
<p>Standard Hindi, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_India" title="Official languages of India">22 officially recognized languages of India</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of the Union, is usually written in the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a> script of India and exhibits less Persian and Arabic influence than Urdu. It has a literature of 500 years, with prose, poetry, religion and philosophy. One could conceive of a wide spectrum of dialects and registers, with the highly Persianised Urdu at one end of the spectrum and a heavily Sanskritised variety spoken in the region around <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a>, at the other end. In common usage in India, the term <i>Hindi</i> includes all these dialects except those at the Urdu spectrum. Thus, the different meanings of the word <i>Hindi</i> include, among others:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ol><li>standardized Hindi as taught in schools throughout India (except some states such as Tamil Nadu),</li>
<li>formal or official Hindi advocated by <a href="/wiki/Purushottam_Das_Tandon" title="Purushottam Das Tandon">Purushottam Das Tandon</a> and as instituted by the post-independence Indian government, heavily influenced by Sanskrit,</li>
<li>the vernacular dialects of Hindustani as spoken throughout India,</li>
<li>the neutralized form of Hindustani used in popular television and films (which is nearly identical to colloquial Urdu), or</li>
<li>the more formal neutralized form of Hindustani used in television and print news reports.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Standard_Urdu">Modern Standard Urdu</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zaban_urdu_mualla.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="225" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Zaban_urdu_mualla.png/165px-Zaban_urdu_mualla.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Zaban_urdu_mualla.png 1.5x" width="165"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zaban_urdu_mualla.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The phrase <i>Zabān-e Urdu-ye Mualla</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasta%CA%BFl%C4%ABq" title="Nastaʿlīq">Nastaʿlīq</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a></div>
<p>Urdu is the <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_language" title="State language">state language</a> of Pakistan and one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_India" title="Official languages of India">22 officially recognised languages of India</a>. 
It is written, except in some parts of India, in the <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" title="Nastaliq">Nastaliq</a> style of the <a href="/wiki/Urdu_alphabet" title="Urdu alphabet">Urdu alphabet</a>, an extended Perso-Arabic script incorporating Indic phonemes. It is heavily influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dari_Persian" title="Dari Persian">Persian</a> vocabulary and was historically also known as <a href="/wiki/Rekhta" title="Rekhta">Rekhta</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="724" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png/200px-Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png/300px-Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png/400px-Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lashkari_Zaban_calligraphy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Lashkari Zabān</i> title in the Perso-Arabic script</div></div></div>
<p>As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakhini" title="Dakhini">Dakhini</a> (or Deccani) where it also draws words from local languages, it survives and enjoys a rich history in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deccan" title="Deccan">Deccan</a> and other parts of <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>, with the prestige dialect being <a href="/wiki/Hyderabadi_Urdu" title="Hyderabadi Urdu">Hyderabadi Urdu</a> spoken in and around the capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nizams_of_Hyderabad" title="Nizams of Hyderabad">Nizams</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deccan_Sultanates" title="Deccan Sultanates">Deccan Sultanates</a>.
</p><p>Earliest forms of the language's literature may be traced back to the 13th-14th century works of <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amīr Khusrau Dehlavī</a>, often called the "father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_literature" title="Urdu literature">Urdu literature</a>" while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wali_Dakhni" title="Wali Dakhni">Walī Deccani</a> is seen as the progenitor of <a href="/wiki/Urdu_poetry" title="Urdu poetry">Urdu poetry</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bazaar_Hindustani">Bazaar Hindustani</span></h3>
<p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Bazaar" title="Bazaar">bazaar</a> Hindustani</i>, in other words, the 'street talk' or literally 'marketplace Hindustani', has arisen to denote a colloquial register of the language that uses vocabulary common to both Hindi and Urdu while eschewing high-register and specialized Arabic or Sanskrit derived words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> It has emerged in various South Asian cities where Hindustani is not the main language, in order to facilitate communication across language barriers. It is characterized by loanwords from local languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir_Khusro" title="Amir Khusro">Amir Khusro</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1300</span> referred to this language of his writings as <i>Dehlavi</i> (<span title="Indic languages collective text"><span lang="inc-Deva">देहलवी</span></span> / <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">دہلوی</span></span></span>, 'of Delhi') or <i>Hindavi</i> (<span title="Indic languages collective text"><span lang="inc-Deva">हिन्दवी</span></span> / <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">ہندوی</span></span></span>). During this period, Hindustani was used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufis" title="Sufis">Sufis</a> in promulgating their message across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amri-Hindavi_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amri-Hindavi-84">[81]</a></sup> After the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a> in the subcontinent, Hindustani acquired more Persian loanwords. <i><a href="/wiki/Rekhta" title="Rekhta">Rekhta</a></i> ('mixture'), <i>Hindi</i> ('Indian'), Hindustani, Hindvi, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lahori" title="Lahori">Lahori</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakni" title="Dakni">Dakni</a> (amongst others) became popular names for the same language until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgregor_912_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcgregor_912-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faruqi_806_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-faruqi_806-85">[82]</a></sup> The name <i>Urdu</i> (from <i>Zabān-i-Ordu</i>, or <i>Orda</i>) appeared around 1780.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faruqi_806_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-faruqi_806-85">[82]</a></sup> It is believed to have been coined by the poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mashafi" title="Mashafi">Mashafi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> In local literature and speech, it was also known as the <i>Lashkari Zabān</i> (military language) or <i>Lashkari</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> Mashafi was the first person to simply modify the name <i>Zabān-i-Ordu</i> to <i><a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, the term <i>Hindustani</i> was used by British officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faruqi_806_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-faruqi_806-85">[82]</a></sup> In 1796, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Borthwick_Gilchrist" title="John Borthwick Gilchrist">John Borthwick Gilchrist</a> published a "A Grammar of the Hindoostanee Language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faruqi_806_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-faruqi_806-85">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilchrist_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilchrist-89">[86]</a></sup> Upon <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">partition</a>, India and Pakistan established national standards that they called <i>Hindi</i> and <i>Urdu,</i> respectively, and attempted to make distinct, with the result that <i>Hindustani</i> commonly, but mistakenly, came to be seen as a "mixture" of Hindi and Urdu.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Abraham_Grierson" title="George Abraham Grierson">Grierson</a>, in his highly influential <i><a href="/wiki/Linguistic_Survey_of_India" title="Linguistic Survey of India">Linguistic Survey of India</a></i>, proposed that the names <i>Hindustani, Urdu,</i> and <i>Hindi</i> be separated in use for different varieties of the Hindustani language, rather than as the overlapping synonyms they frequently were:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We may now define the three main varieties of Hindōstānī as follows:—Hindōstānī is primarily the language of the Upper Gangetic Doab, and is also the <i>lingua franca</i> of India, capable of being written in both Persian and Dēva-nāgarī characters, and without purism, avoiding alike the excessive use of either Persian or Sanskrit words when employed for literature. The name 'Urdū' can then be confined to that special variety of Hindōstānī in which Persian words are of frequent occurrence, and which hence can only be written in the Persian character, and, similarly, 'Hindī' can be confined to the form of Hindōstānī in which Sanskrit words abound, and which hence can only be written in the Dēva-nāgarī character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grierson_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grierson-3">[2]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hindi_literature" title="Hindi literature">Hindi literature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_literature" title="Urdu literature">Urdu literature</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_asia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/South_asia.jpg/210px-South_asia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/South_asia.jpg/315px-South_asia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/South_asia.jpg/420px-South_asia.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_asia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hindustani, in its standardised registers, is one of the official languages of both India (Hindi) and Pakistan (Urdu).</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to 1947, Hindustani was officially recognised by the British Raj. In the post-independence period however, the term Hindustani has lost currency and is not given any official recognition by the Indian or Pakistani governments. The language is instead recognised by its standard forms, Hindi and Urdu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindi">Hindi</span></h3>
<p>Hindi is declared by Article 343(1), <a href="/wiki/Part_XVII_of_the_Constitution_of_India" title="Part XVII of the Constitution of India">Part 17</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Indian Constitution</a> as the "official language (<span title="Indic languages collective text"><span lang="inc-Deva">राजभाषा</span></span>, <span title="ISO 15919 Indic (Indic languages language) transliteration"><i lang="inc-Latn">rājabhāṣā</i></span>) of the Union." (In this context, "Union" means the Federal Government and not the entire country<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>—India has <a href="/wiki/Languages_with_official_status_in_India" title="Languages with official status in India">23 official languages</a>.) At the same time, however, the definitive text of federal laws is officially the English text and proceedings in the higher appellate courts must be conducted in English.
</p><p>At the state level, Hindi is one of the official languages in 10 of the 29 Indian states and three <a href="/wiki/Union_territory" title="Union territory">Union Territories</a>, respectively: <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chhattisgarh" title="Chhattisgarh">Chhattisgarh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>; <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dadra_and_Nagar_Haveli" title="Dadra and Nagar Haveli">Dadra and Nagar Haveli</a>, and Delhi.
</p><p>In the remaining states, Hindi is not an official language. In states like <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, studying Hindi is not compulsory in the state curriculum. However, an option to take the same as second or third language does exist. In many other states, studying Hindi is usually compulsory in the school curriculum as a third language (the first two languages being the state's official language and English), though the intensiveness of Hindi in the curriculum varies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urdu">Urdu</span></h3>
<p>Urdu is the national language (<span title="Urdu-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ur"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">قومی زبان</span></span></span>, <i>qaumi zabān</i>) of Pakistan, where it shares <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> status with <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_English" title="Pakistani English">English</a>. Although English is spoken by many, and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> is the native language of the majority of the population, Urdu is the <i>lingua franca</i>. In India, Urdu is one of the languages recognised in the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Schedule_to_the_Constitution_of_India" title="Eighth Schedule to the Constitution of India">Eighth Schedule to the Constitution of India</a> and is an official language of the Indian states of <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, and also the Union Territories of Delhi and Jammu and Kashmir. Although the government school system in most other states emphasises Modern Standard Hindi, at universities in cities such as <a href="/wiki/Lucknow" title="Lucknow">Lucknow</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aligarh,_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Aligarh, Uttar Pradesh">Aligarh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyderabad" title="Hyderabad">Hyderabad</a>, Urdu is spoken and learnt, and <i>Saaf</i> or <i>Khaalis</i> Urdu is treated with just as much respect as <i>Shuddha</i> Hindi.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span></h2>
<p>Besides being the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of North India and Pakistan in South Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-siddiqi1994-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ashmore1961-39">[36]</a></sup> Hindustani is also spoken by many in the South Asian diaspora and their descendants around the world, including <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> (e.g., in Canada, Hindustani is one of the fastest growing languages),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.
</p>
<ul><li>A sizeable population in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, can also speak and understand Hindi-Urdu due to the popularity and influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> films and songs in the region, as well as the fact that many Afghan refugees spent time in Pakistan in the 1980s and 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hakala2012_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hakala2012-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krishnamurthy2013_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krishnamurthy2013-94">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fiji_Hindi" title="Fiji Hindi">Fiji Hindi</a> was derived from the Hindustani linguistic group and is spoken widely by Fijians of <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indian</a> origin.</li>
<li>Hindustani was also one of the languages that was spoken widely during <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Burma" title="British rule in Burma">British rule in Burma</a>. Many older citizens of <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Indian" title="Anglo-Indian">Anglo-Indians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_people" title="Anglo-Burmese people">Anglo-Burmese</a>, still know it, although it has had no official status in the country since <a href="/wiki/Myanmar#Military_rule_(1962–2011)" title="Myanmar">military rule</a> began.</li>
<li>Hindustani is also spoken in the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Migrant_worker" title="Migrant worker">migrant workers</a> from various countries live and work for several years.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_phonology" title="Hindustani phonology">Hindustani phonology</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">Hindustani grammar</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_etymology" title="Hindustani etymology">Hindustani etymology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_vocabulary" title="Hindustani vocabulary">Hindustani vocabulary</a></div>
<p>Hindi-Urdu's <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Core_vocabulary&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Core vocabulary (page does not exist)">core vocabulary</a> has an Indic base, being derived from <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a>, which in turn derives from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-GubeGao2019-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper2010-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997-43">[40]</a></sup> as well as a substantial amount of <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_etymology" title="Hindustani etymology">loanwords</a> from <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (via Persian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JainCardona2007_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-JainCardona2007-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper2003_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Draper2003-44">[41]</a></sup> Hindustani contains around 5,500 words of Persian and Arabic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_orthography" title="Hindustani orthography">Hindustani orthography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devanagari_Braille" title="Devanagari Braille">Devanagari Braille</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urdu_Braille" title="Urdu Braille">Urdu Braille</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surahi_in_samrup_rachna_calligraphy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Surahi_in_samrup_rachna_calligraphy.jpg" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surahi_in_samrup_rachna_calligraphy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Surahi" in <a href="/wiki/Samrup_Rachna" title="Samrup Rachna">Samrup Rachna</a> calligraphy</div></div></div>
<p>Historically, Hindustani was written in the <a href="/wiki/Kaithi" title="Kaithi">Kaithi</a>, Devanagari, and Urdu alphabets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgregor_912_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-mcgregor_912-69">[66]</a></sup> Kaithi and Devanagari are two of the <a href="/wiki/Brahmic_scripts" title="Brahmic scripts">Brahmic scripts</a> native to India, whereas the Urdu alphabet is a derivation of the Perso-Arabic script written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasta%CA%BFl%C4%ABq" title="Nastaʿlīq">Nastaʿlīq</a>, which is the preferred calligraphic style for Urdu.
</p><p>Today, Hindustani continues to be written in the Urdu alphabet in Pakistan. In India, the Hindi register is officially written in Devanagari, and Urdu in the Urdu alphabet, to the extent that these standards are partly defined by their script.
</p><p>However, in popular publications in India, Urdu is also written in Devanagari, with slight variations to establish a Devanagari Urdu alphabet alongside the Devanagari Hindi alphabet.
</p>

<td><i>ye</i></td>
<td>y or ī</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_approximant" title="Voiced palatal approximant">j</a>/</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">iː</a>/</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Urdu-language text"><span lang="ur"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">ے</span></span></span></td>
<td><i>baṛī ye</i></td>
<td>ai or ē</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛː</a>/</span>, or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">eː</a>/</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Because of <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">anglicisation</a> in South Asia and the international use of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, Hindustani is occasionally written in the Latin script. This adaptation is called <a href="/wiki/Roman_Urdu" title="Roman Urdu">Roman Urdu</a> or Romanised Hindi, depending upon the register used. Since Urdu and Hindi are <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a> when spoken, Romanised Hindi and Roman Urdu (unlike Devanagari Hindi and Urdu in the Urdu alphabet) are mostly mutually intelligible as well.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colloquial_Hindustani">Colloquial Hindustani</span></h3>
<p>An example of colloquial Hindustani:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelacyAhmed2005_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-DelacyAhmed2005-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Devanagari</b>: <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span lang="inc-Deva">यह कितने का है?</span></span></li>
<li><b>Urdu</b>: <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">یہ کتنے کا ہے؟</span></span></span></li>
<li><b>Romanisation</b>: <span title="ISO 15919 Indic (Indic languages language) transliteration"><i lang="inc-Latn">Yah kitnē kā hai?</i></span></li>
<li><b>English</b>: How much is it?</li></ul>
<p>The following is a sample text, Article 1 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, in the two official registers of Hindustani, Hindi and Urdu. Because this is a formal legal text, differences in vocabulary are most pronounced.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_Hindi">Literary Hindi</span></h3>
<p><span title="Hindi-language text"><span lang="hi">अनुच्छेद १ — सभी मनुष्यों को गौरव और अधिकारों के विषय में जन्मजात स्वतन्त्रता और समानता प्राप्त हैं। उन्हें बुद्धि और अन्तरात्मा की देन प्राप्त है और परस्पर उन्हें भाईचारे के भाव से बर्ताव करना चाहिए।</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Urdu transliteration
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Hindi-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hi-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">انُچھید ١ : سبھی منُشیوں کو گورو اور ادھکاروں کے وِشئے میں جنمجات سوَتنتْرتا پراپت ہیں۔ اُنہیں بدھی اور انتراتما کی دین پراپت ہے اور پرسپر اُنہیں بھائی چارے کے بھاؤ سے برتاؤ کرنا چاہئے۔</span></span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Transliteration (ISO 15919)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="ISO 15919 Indic (Hindi language) transliteration"><i lang="hi-Latn">Anucchēd 1: Sabhī manuṣyō̃ kō gaurav aur adhikārō̃ kē viṣay mē̃ janmajāt svatantratā aur samāntā prāpt haĩ. Unhē̃ buddhi aur antarātmā kī dēn prāpt hai aur paraspar unhē̃ bhāīcārē kē bhāv sē bartāv karnā cāhiē.</i></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>

</th></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Gloss (word-to-word)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Article 1—<i>All</i> human-beings to dignity and rights' matter in from-birth freedom acquired is. Them to reason and conscience's endowment acquired is and always them to brotherhood's spirit with behaviour to do should.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Translation (grammatical)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Article 1—All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_Urdu">Literary Urdu</span></h3>
<p><span title="Urdu-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ur-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">:دفعہ ١: تمام اِنسان آزاد اور حُقوق و عِزت کے اعتبار سے برابر پَیدا ہُوئے ہَیں۔ انہیں ضمِیر اور عقل ودِیعت ہوئی ہَیں۔ اِس لئے انہیں ایک دُوسرے کے ساتھ بھائی چارے کا سُلُوک کرنا چاہئے۔</span></span></span>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindustani_and_Bollywood">Hindustani and Bollywood</span></h2>
<p>The predominant Indian film industry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Hindi" title="Modern Standard Hindi">Modern Standard Hindi</a>, colloquial Hindustani, <a href="/wiki/Bombay_Hindi" title="Bombay Hindi">Bombay Hindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scienceandmediamuseum_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-scienceandmediamuseum-98">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Awadhi_language" title="Awadhi language">Awadhi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rajasthani_language" title="Rajasthani language">Rajasthani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri_language" title="Bhojpuri language">Bhojpuri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Braj_Bhasha" title="Braj Bhasha">Braj Bhasha</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> and with the liberal use of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hinglish" title="Hinglish">Hinglish</a> in scripts and soundtrack lyrics.
</p><p>Film titles are often screened in three scripts: Latin, Devanagari and occasionally Perso-Arabic. The use of Urdu or Hindi in films depends on the film's context: historical films set in the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> are almost entirely in Urdu, whereas films based on <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Middle_kingdoms_of_India" title="Middle kingdoms of India">ancient India</a> make heavy use of Hindi with Sanskrit vocabulary.
</p>
