</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This will only display in a <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" title="Nastaliq">Nastaliq</a> font if you will have one installed, otherwise it may display in a modern <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic_font&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arabic font (page does not exist)">Arabic font</a> in a style more common for writing <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and most other non-Urdu languages such as <a href="/wiki/Naskh_(script)" title="Naskh (script)">Naskh</a>. If this <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq"><span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran">پاکستان</span></span></span> and this <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">پاکستان</span></span></span> looks like this <span title="Indic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="inc-Aran">پاکستان</span></span> then you are not seeing it in Nastaliq.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ethnologue-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ethnologue_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ethnologue_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"Hindi" L1: 322 million (2011 Indian census), including perhaps 150 million speakers of other languages that reported their language as "Hindi" on the census. L2: 274 million (2016, source unknown). Urdu L1: 67 million (2011 &amp; 2017 censuses), L2: 102 million (1999 Pakistan, source unknown, and 2001 Indian census):  <i>Ethnologue</i> 21. <a class="external text" href="https://www.ethnologue.com/21/language/hin" rel="nofollow">Hindi</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> (21st ed., 2018). <a class="external text" href="https://www.ethnologue.com/21/language/ur" rel="nofollow">Urdu</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> (21st ed., 2018).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Grierson-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Grierson_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Grierson_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Grierson_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Grierson_3-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Grierson, vol. 9–1, p. 47. <i>We may now define the three main varieties of Hindōstānī as follows:—Hindōstānī is primarily the language of the Upper Gangetic Doab, and is also the </i>lingua franca<i> of India, capable of being written in both Persian and Dēva-nāgarī characters, and without purism, avoiding alike the excessive use of either Persian or Sanskrit words when employed for literature. The name 'Urdū' can then be confined to that special variety of Hindōstānī in which Persian words are of frequent occurrence, and which hence can only be written in the Persian character, and, similarly, 'Hindī' can be confined to the form of Hindōstānī in which Sanskrit words abound, and which hence can only be written in the Dēva-nāgarī character.</i></span>
</li>

<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2">"हिंदुस्तानी", <a class="external text" href="http://www.chdpublication.mhrd.gov.in/ebook/b101/html5forpc.html?page=0" rel="nofollow"><i>बृहत हिंदी कोश खंड 2 (Large Hindi Dictionary, Volume 2)</i></a>, केन्द्रीय हिंदी निदेशालय, भारत सरकार (Central Hindi Directorate, Government of India), p. 1458<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">17 October</span> 2021</span></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%81%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%A4%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%A8%E0%A5%80&amp;rft.btitle=%E0%A4%AC%E0%A5%83%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%A4+%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%80+%E0%A4%95%E0%A5%8B%E0%A4%B6+%E0%A4%96%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%A1+2+%28Large+Hindi+Dictionary%2C+Volume+2%29&amp;rft.pages=1458&amp;rft.pub=%E0%A4%95%E0%A5%87%E0%A4%A8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B0%E0%A5%80%E0%A4%AF+%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%80+%E0%A4%A8%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%87%E0%A4%B6%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%B2%E0%A4%AF%2C+%E0%A4%AD%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%A4+%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%B0%E0%A4%95%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%B0+%28Central+Hindi+Directorate%2C+Government+of+India%29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.chdpublication.mhrd.gov.in%2Febook%2Fb101%2Fhtml5forpc.html%3Fpage%3D0&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFDas1975">Das, Shyamasundar (1975), <a class="external text" href="https://dsal.uchicago.edu/cgi-bin/app/dasa-hindi_query.py?page=5505" rel="nofollow"><i>Hindi Shabda Sagar (Hindi dictionary) in 11 volumes, revised edition</i></a>, Kashi (Varanasi): <a href="/wiki/Nagari_Pracharini_Sabha" title="Nagari Pracharini Sabha">Nagari Pracharini Sabha</a>, p. 5505, <q>हिंदुस्तानी hindustānī३ संज्ञा स्त्री॰ १. हिंदुस्तान की भाषा । २. बोलचाल या व्यवहार की वह हिंदी जिसमें न तो बहुत अरबी फारसी के शब्द हों न संस्कृत के । उ॰—साहिब लोगों ने इस देश की भाषा का एक नया नाम हिंदुस्तानी रखा । Translation: Hindustani hindustānī3 noun feminine 1. The language of Hindustan. 2. That version of  Hindi employed for common speech or business in which neither many Arabic or Persian words nor Sanskrit words are present. Context: The British gave the new name Hindustani to the language of this country.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hindi+Shabda+Sagar+%28Hindi+dictionary%29+in+11+volumes%2C+revised+edition&amp;rft.place=Kashi+%28Varanasi%29&amp;rft.pages=5505&amp;rft.pub=Nagari+Pracharini+Sabha&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft.aulast=Das&amp;rft.aufirst=Shyamasundar&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdsal.uchicago.edu%2Fcgi-bin%2Fapp%2Fdasa-hindi_query.py%3Fpage%3D5505&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFChaturvedi1970">Chaturvedi, Mahendra (1970), "हिंदुस्तानी", <i>A Practical Hindi-English Dictionary</i>, Delhi: National Publishing House, <q>hindustānī hīndusta:nī:  a theoretically existent style of the Hindi language which is supposed to consist of current and simple words of any sources whatever and is neither too much biassed in favour of Perso-Arabic elements nor has any place for too much high-flown Sanskritized vocabulary</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%82%E0%A4%A6%E0%A5%81%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%A4%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%A8%E0%A5%80&amp;rft.btitle=A+Practical+Hindi-English+Dictionary&amp;rft.place=Delhi&amp;rft.pub=National+Publishing+House&amp;rft.date=1970&amp;rft.aulast=Chaturvedi&amp;rft.aufirst=Mahendra&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-NCSU-Hindustani-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NCSU-Hindustani_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090815023328/http://sasw.chass.ncsu.edu/fl/faculty/taj/hindi/abturdu.htm" rel="nofollow">"About Hindi-Urdu"</a>. <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_University" title="North Carolina State University">North Carolina State University</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://sasw.chass.ncsu.edu/fl/faculty/taj/hindi/abturdu.htm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 15 August 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-siddiqi1994-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-siddiqi1994_14-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFMohammad_Tahsin_Siddiqi1994">Mohammad Tahsin Siddiqi (1994), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=vnrTAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Hindustani-English code-mixing in modern literary texts</i></a>, University of Wisconsin, <q>... Hindustani is the lingua franca of both India and Pakistan ...</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hindustani-English+code-mixing+in+modern+literary+texts&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Wisconsin&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.au=Mohammad+Tahsin+Siddiqi&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DvnrTAAAAMAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-britannica-2018-hindustani-language-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-britannica-2018-hindustani-language_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hindustani-language" rel="nofollow">"Hindustani language"</a>. Encyclopedia Britannica. 1 November 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2021</span>. <q>(subscription required) lingua franca of northern India and Pakistan. Two variants of Hindustani, Urdu and Hindi, are official languages in Pakistan and India, respectively. Hindustani began to develop during the 13th century CE in and around the Indian cities of Delhi and Meerut in response to the increasing linguistic diversity that resulted from Muslim hegemony. In the 19th century its use was widely promoted by the British, who initiated an effort at standardization. Hindustani is widely recognized as India’s most common lingua franca, but its status as a vernacular renders it difficult to measure precisely its number of speakers.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Hindustani+language&amp;rft.pub=Encyclopedia+Britannica&amp;rft.date=2018-11-01&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2Ftopic%2FHindustani-language&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-trask-hclinguistics-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-trask-hclinguistics_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFTrask2019">Trask, R. L. (8 August 2019), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=jacxEAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA149" rel="nofollow">"Hindi-Urdu"</a>, <i>Dictionary of Historical and Comparative Linguistics</i>, Edinburgh University Press, pp. 149–150, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781474473316" title="Special:BookSources/9781474473316"><bdi>9781474473316</bdi></a>, <q><b>Hindi-Urdu</b> The most important modern Indo-Aryan language, spoken by well over 250 million people, mainly in India and Pakistan.  At the spoken level <i>Hindi</i> and <i>Urdu</i> are the same language (called <i>Hindustani</i> before the political partition), but the two varieties are written in different alphabets and differ substantially in their abstract and technical vocabularies</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Hindi-Urdu&amp;rft.btitle=Dictionary+of+Historical+and+Comparative+Linguistics&amp;rft.pages=149-150&amp;rft.pub=Edinburgh+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2019-08-08&amp;rft.isbn=9781474473316&amp;rft.aulast=Trask&amp;rft.aufirst=R.+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DjacxEAAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA149&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-dcrystal-dict-lang-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-dcrystal-dict-lang_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFCrystal2001"><a href="/wiki/David_Crystal" title="David Crystal">Crystal, David</a> (2001), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=3GU5FWs1pBEC" rel="nofollow"><i>A Dictionary of Language</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago_Press" title="University of Chicago Press">University of Chicago Press</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780226122038" title="Special:BookSources/9780226122038"><bdi>9780226122038</bdi></a>, <q>(p. 115) Figure: A family of languages: the Indo-European family tree, reflecting geographical distribution. Proto Indo-European&gt;Indo-Iranian&gt;Indo-Aryan (Sanskrit)&gt; Midland (Rajasthani, Bihari, Hindi/Urdu); (p. 149) <b>Hindi</b> There is little structural difference between Hindi and Urdu, and the two are often grouped together under the single label Hindi/Urdu, sometimes abbreviated to Hirdu, and formerly often called Hindustani; (p. 160) <b>India</b> ... With such linguistic diversity, Hindi/Urdu has come to be widely used as a lingua franca.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Dictionary+of+Language&amp;rft.place=Chicago&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=9780226122038&amp;rft.aulast=Crystal&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D3GU5FWs1pBEC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-gandhi-hindi-urdu-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-gandhi-hindi-urdu_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGandhi2018"><a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Gandhi, M. K.</a> (2018). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=h5BNDwAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>An Autobiography or The Story of My Experiments with Truth: A Critical Edition</i></a>. Translated by <a href="/wiki/Mahadev_Desai" title="Mahadev Desai">Desai, Mahadev</a>. annotation by Suhrud, Tridip. <a href="/wiki/New_Haven,_Connecticut" title="New Haven, Connecticut">New Haven</a> and London: <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Press" title="Yale University Press">Yale University Press</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780300234077" title="Special:BookSources/9780300234077"><bdi>9780300234077</bdi></a>. <q>(p. 737) I was handicapped for want of suitable Hindi or Urdu words. This was my first occasion for delivering an argumentative speech before an audience especially composed of Mussalmans of the North. I had spoken in Urdu at the Muslim League at Calcutta, but it was only for a few minutes, and the speech was intended only to be a feeling appeal to the audience. Here, on the contrary, I was faced with a critical, if not hostile, audience, to whom I had to explain and bring home my view-point. But I had cast aside all shyness. I was not there to deliver an address in the faultless, polished Urdu of the Delhi Muslims, but to place before the gathering my views in such broken Hindi as I could command. And in this I was successful. This meeting afforded me a direct proof of the fact that Hindi-Urdu alone could become the lingua franca&lt;Footnote M8&gt; of India. (M8: "national language" in the Gujarati original).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=An+Autobiography+or+The+Story+of+My+Experiments+with+Truth%3A+A+Critical+Edition&amp;rft.place=New+Haven+and+London&amp;rft.pub=Yale+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft.isbn=9780300234077&amp;rft.aulast=Gandhi&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+K.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dh5BNDwAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Basu2017-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Basu2017_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Basu2017_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Basu2017_19-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBasu2017">Basu, Manisha (2017). <i>The Rhetoric of Hindutva</i>. <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-14987-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-107-14987-8"><bdi>978-1-107-14987-8</bdi></a>. <q>Urdu, like Hindi, was a standardized register of the Hindustani language deriving from the Dehlavi dialect and emerged in the eighteenth century under the rule of the late Mughals.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Rhetoric+of+Hindutva&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2017&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-107-14987-8&amp;rft.aulast=Basu&amp;rft.aufirst=Manisha&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-GubeGao2019-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GubeGao2019_20-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGubeGao2019">Gube, Jan; Gao, Fang (2019). <i>Education, Ethnicity and Equity in the Multilingual Asian Context</i>. <a href="/wiki/Springer_Publishing" title="Springer Publishing">Springer Publishing</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-981-13-3125-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-981-13-3125-1"><bdi>978-981-13-3125-1</bdi></a>. <q>The national language of India and Pakistan 'Standard Urdu' is mutually intelligible with 'Standard Hindi' because both languages share the same Indic base and are all but indistinguishable in phonology and grammar (Lust et al. 2000).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Education%2C+Ethnicity+and+Equity+in+the+Multilingual+Asian+Context&amp;rft.pub=Springer+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2019&amp;rft.isbn=978-981-13-3125-1&amp;rft.aulast=Gube&amp;rft.aufirst=Jan&amp;rft.au=Gao%2C+Fang&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-pulsipher2005-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-pulsipher2005_21-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFLydia_Mihelič_PulsipherAlex_PulsipherHolly_M._Hapke2005">Lydia Mihelič Pulsipher; Alex Pulsipher; Holly M. Hapke (2005), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WfNaSNNAppQC" rel="nofollow"><i>World Regional Geography: Global Patterns, Local Lives</i></a>, Macmillan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-1904-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7167-1904-5"><bdi>978-0-7167-1904-5</bdi></a>, <q>... By the time of British colonialism, Hindustani was the <i>lingua franca</i> of all of northern India and what is today Pakistan ...</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=World+Regional+Geography%3A+Global+Patterns%2C+Local+Lives&amp;rft.pub=Macmillan&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7167-1904-5&amp;rft.au=Lydia+Miheli%C4%8D+Pulsipher&amp;rft.au=Alex+Pulsipher&amp;rft.au=Holly+M.+Hapke&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWfNaSNNAppQC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Elsevier2010-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Elsevier2010_22-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>Concise Encyclopedia of Languages of the World</i>. Elsevier. 2010. p. 497. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-08-087775-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-08-087775-4"><bdi>978-0-08-087775-4</bdi></a>. <q>Hindustani is a Central Indo-Aryan language based on Khari Boli (Khaṛi Boli). Its origin, development, and function reflect the dynamics of the sociolinguistic contact situation from which it emerged as a colloquial speech. It is inextricably linked with the emergence and standardisation of Urdu and Hindi.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Concise+Encyclopedia+of+Languages+of+the+World&amp;rft.pages=497&amp;rft.pub=Elsevier&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-08-087775-4&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:0-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:0_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFRahman2011">Rahman, Tariq (2011). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141010094507/http://www.tariqrahman.net/content/hindiurdu1.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>From Hindi to Urdu: A Social and Political History</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFBhatBhatJainSharma2016">Bhat, Riyaz Ahmad; Bhat, Irshad Ahmad; Jain, Naman; Sharma, Dipti Misra (2016). <a class="external text" href="http://irshadbhat.github.io/papers-pdf/house-united.pdf" rel="nofollow">"A House United: Bridging the Script and Lexical Barrier between Hindi and Urdu"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Proceedings of COLING 2016, the 26th International Conference on Computational Linguistics<span class="reference-accessdate">. It has been seen to break the barrier that makes the two look different.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=A+House+United%3A+Bridging+the+Script+and+Lexical+Barrier+between+Hindi+and+Urdu&amp;rft.pub=Proceedings+of+COLING+2016%2C+the+26th+International+Conference+on+Computational+Linguistics&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.aulast=Bhat&amp;rft.aufirst=Riyaz+Ahmad&amp;rft.au=Bhat%2C+Irshad+Ahmad&amp;rft.au=Jain%2C+Naman&amp;rft.au=Sharma%2C+Dipti+Misra&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Firshadbhat.github.io%2Fpapers-pdf%2Fhouse-united.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Rekhta2020-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Rekhta2020_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rekhta2020_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://artsandculture.google.com/exhibit/women-of-the-indian-sub-continent-makings-of-a-culture-rekhta-foundation/dwJy7qboNi3fIg?hl=en" rel="nofollow">"Women of the Indian Sub-Continent: Makings of a Culture - Rekhta Foundation"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Google_Arts_%26_Culture" title="Google Arts &amp; Culture">Google Arts &amp; Culture</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 February</span> 2020</span>. <q>The "Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb" is one such instance of the composite culture that marks various regions of the country.  Prevalent in the North, particularly in the central plains, it is born of the union between the Hindu and Muslim cultures. Most of the temples were lined along the Ganges and the Khanqah (Sufi school of thought) were situated along the Yamuna river (also called Jamuna). Thus, it came to be known as the Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb, with the word "tehzeeb" meaning culture. More than communal harmony, its most beautiful by-product was "Hindustani" which later gave us the Hindi and Urdu languages.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Women+of+the+Indian+Sub-Continent%3A+Makings+of+a+Culture+-+Rekhta+Foundation&amp;rft.pub=Google+Arts+%26+Culture&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fartsandculture.google.com%2Fexhibit%2Fwomen-of-the-indian-sub-continent-makings-of-a-culture-rekhta-foundation%2FdwJy7qboNi3fIg%3Fhl%3Den&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>

This variety of Hindi is called Khari Boli, 'the upright speech'.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Urdu+literature&amp;rft.pub=Urdu+Markaz%3B+Third+World+Foundation+for+Social+and+Economic+Studies&amp;rft.date=1985&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-907962-30-4&amp;rft.aulast=Matthews&amp;rft.aufirst=David+John&amp;rft.au=Shackle%2C+C.&amp;rft.au=Husain%2C+Shahanara&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Dhulipala2000-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dhulipala2000_33-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dhulipala2000_33-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDhulipala2000">Dhulipala, Venkat (2000). <i>The Politics of Secularism: Medieval Indian Historiography and the Sufis</i>. <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a>. p. 27. <q>Persian became the court language, and many Persian words crept into popular usage. <li id="cite_note-IJSW1943-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-IJSW1943_34-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-IJSW1943_34-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>Indian Journal of Social Work, Volume 4</i>. <a href="/wiki/Tata_Institute_of_Social_Sciences" title="Tata Institute of Social Sciences">Tata Institute of Social Sciences</a>. 1943. p. 264. <q>... more words of Sanskrit origin but 75% of the vocabulary is common. Urdu is a national language evolved through years of Hindu and Muslim cultural contact and, as stated by Pandit Jawaharlal Nehru, is essentially an Indian language and has no place outside.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Indian+Journal+of+Social+Work%2C+Volume+4&amp;rft.pages=264&amp;rft.pub=Tata+Institute+of+Social+Sciences&amp;rft.date=1943&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Kesavan1997-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kesavan1997_36-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kesavan1997_36-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKesavan1997">Kesavan, B. <li id="cite_note-Hock1991-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hock1991_37-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHans_Henrich_Hock1991"><a href="/wiki/Hans_Henrich_Hock" title="Hans Henrich Hock">Hans Henrich Hock</a> (1991). <i>Principles of Historical Linguistics</i>. Walter de Gruyter. p. 475. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-11-012962-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-11-012962-5"><bdi>978-3-11-012962-5</bdi></a>. <q>During the time of British rule, Hindi (in its religiously neutral, 'Hindustani' variety) increasingly came to be the symbol of national unity over against the English of the foreign oppressor. And Hindustani was learned widely throughout India, even in Bengal and the Dravidian south. ... Independence had been accompanied by the division of former British India into two countries, Pakistan and India. The former had been established as a Muslim state and had made Urdu, the Muslim variety of Hindi–Urdu or Hindustani, its national language.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Principles+of+Historical+Linguistics&amp;rft.pages=475&amp;rft.pub=Walter+de+Gruyter&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-11-012962-5&amp;rft.au=Hans+Henrich+Hock&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-:1-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:1_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMasica1993">Masica, Colin P. (1993). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=J3RSHWePhXwC&amp;q=masica" rel="nofollow"><i>The Indo-Aryan Languages</i></a>. Cambridge University Press. pp. 430 (Appendix I). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-29944-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-29944-2"><bdi>978-0-521-29944-2</bdi></a>. <q>Hindustani - term referring to common colloquial base of HINDI and URDU and to its function as lingua franca over much of India, much in vogue during Independence movement as expression of national unity; after Partition in 1947 and subsequent linguistic polarization it fell into disfavor; census of 1951 registered an enormous decline (86-98 per cent) in no. of persons declaring it their mother tongue (the majority of HINDI speakers and many URDU speakers had done so in previous censuses); trend continued in subsequent censuses: only 11,053 returned it in 1971...mostly from S India; [see Khubchandani 1983: 90-1].</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Indo-Aryan+Languages&amp;rft.pages=430+%28Appendix+I%29&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-29944-2&amp;rft.aulast=Masica&amp;rft.aufirst=Colin+P.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DJ3RSHWePhXwC%26q%3Dmasica&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Ashmore1961-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ashmore1961_39-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAshmore1961">Ashmore, Harry S. (1961). <i>Encyclopaedia Britannica: a new survey of universal knowledge, Volume 11</i>. <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a>. p. 579. <q>The everyday speech of well over 50,000,000 persons of all communities in the north of India and in West Pakistan is the expression of a common language, Hindustani.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopaedia+Britannica%3A+a+new+survey+of+universal+knowledge%2C+Volume+11&amp;rft.pages=579&amp;rft.pub=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica&amp;rft.date=1961&amp;rft.aulast=Ashmore&amp;rft.aufirst=Harry+S.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Tunstall2008-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Tunstall2008_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFTunstall2008">Tunstall, Jeremy (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/mediawereamerica0000tuns/page/160" rel="nofollow"><i>The media were American: U.S. mass media in decline</i></a>. Oxford University Press. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/mediawereamerica0000tuns/page/160" rel="nofollow">160</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518146-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518146-3"><bdi>978-0-19-518146-3</bdi></a>. <q>The Hindi film industry used the most popular street level version of Hindi, namely Hindustani, which included a lot of Urdu and Persian words.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+media+were+American%3A+U.S.+mass+media+in+decline&amp;rft.pages=160&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-518146-3&amp;rft.aulast=Tunstall&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeremy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fmediawereamerica0000tuns%2Fpage%2F160&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Hiro2015-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hiro2015_41-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hiro2015_41-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHiro2015">Hiro, Dilip (2015). <i>The Longest August: The Unflinching Rivalry Between India and Pakistan</i>. <a href="/wiki/PublicAffairs" title="PublicAffairs">PublicAffairs</a>. p. 398. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-56858-503-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-56858-503-1"><bdi>978-1-56858-503-1</bdi></a>. <q>Spoken Hindi is akin to spoken Urdu, and that language is often called Hindustani. Bollywood's screenplays are written in Hindustani.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Longest+August%3A+The+Unflinching+Rivalry+Between+India+and+Pakistan&amp;rft.pages=398&amp;rft.pub=PublicAffairs&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-56858-503-1&amp;rft.aulast=Hiro&amp;rft.aufirst=Dilip&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Kuiper2010-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kuiper2010_42-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKuiper2010">Kuiper, Kathleen (2010). <i>The Culture of India</i>. <a href="/wiki/Rosen_Publishing" title="Rosen Publishing">Rosen Publishing</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-61530-149-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-61530-149-2"><bdi>978-1-61530-149-2</bdi></a>. <q>Urdu is closely related to Hindi, a language that originated and developed in the Indian subcontinent. They share the same Indic base and are so similar in phonology and grammar that they appear to be one language.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Culture+of+India&amp;rft.pub=Rosen+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-61530-149-2&amp;rft.aulast=Kuiper&amp;rft.aufirst=Kathleen&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997_43-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997_43-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFChatterjiSiṃhaPadikkal1997">Chatterji, Suniti Kumar; Siṃha, Udaẏa Nārāẏana; Padikkal, Shivarama (1997). <i>Suniti Kumar Chatterji: a centenary tribute</i>. Sahitya Akademi. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-260-0353-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-260-0353-2"><bdi>978-81-260-0353-2</bdi></a>. <q>High Hindi written in Devanagari, having identical grammar with Urdu, employing the native Hindi or Hindustani (Prakrit) elements to the fullest, but for words of high culture, going to Sanskrit. Hindustani proper that represents the basic Khari Boli with vocabulary holding a balance between Urdu and High Hindi.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Suniti+Kumar+Chatterji%3A+a+centenary+tribute&amp;rft.pub=Sahitya+Akademi&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=978-81-260-0353-2&amp;rft.aulast=Chatterji&amp;rft.aufirst=Suniti+Kumar&amp;rft.au=Si%E1%B9%83ha%2C+Uda%E1%BA%8Fa+N%C4%81r%C4%81%E1%BA%8Fana&amp;rft.au=Padikkal%2C+Shivarama&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Draper2003-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Draper2003_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Draper2003_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDraper2003">Draper, Allison Stark (2003). <i>India: A Primary Source Cultural Guide</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosen_Publishing_Group" title="Rosen Publishing Group">Rosen Publishing Group</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8239-3838-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8239-3838-4"><bdi>978-0-8239-3838-4</bdi></a>. <q>People in Delhi spoke Khari Boli, a language the British called Hindustani. It used an Indo-Aryan grammatical structure and numerous Persian "loan-words."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=India%3A+A+Primary+Source+Cultural+Guide&amp;rft.pub=Rosen+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8239-3838-4&amp;rft.aulast=Draper&amp;rft.aufirst=Allison+Stark&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Ahmad2002-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ahmad2002_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAhmad2002">Ahmad, Aijaz (2002). <i>Lineages of the Present: Ideology and Politics in Contemporary South Asia</i>. Verso. p. 113. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781859843581" title="Special:BookSources/9781859843581"><bdi>9781859843581</bdi></a>. <q>On this there are far more reliable statistics than those on population. <i>Farhang-e-Asafiya</i> is by general agreement the most reliable Urdu dictionary. It twas compiled in the late nineteenth century by an Indian scholar little exposed to British or Orientalist scholarship. The lexicographer in question, Syed Ahmed Dehlavi, had no desire to sunder Urdu's relationship with Farsi, as is evident even from the title of his dictionary. He estimates that roughly 75 per cent of the total stock of 55,000 Urdu words that he compiled in his dictionary are derived from Sanskrit and Prakrit, and that the entire stock of the base words of the language, without exception, are derived from these sources. What distinguishes Urdu from a great many other Indian languauges ... is that is draws almost a quarter of its vocabulary from language communities to the west of India, such as Farsi, Turkish, and Tajik. Most of the little it takes from Arabic has not come directly but through Farsi.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Lineages+of+the+Present%3A+Ideology+and+Politics+in+Contemporary+South+Asia&amp;rft.pages=113&amp;rft.pub=Verso&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=9781859843581&amp;rft.aulast=Ahmad&amp;rft.aufirst=Aijaz&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Dalmia2017-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dalmia2017_46-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDalmia2017">Dalmia, Vasudha (31 July 2017). <i>Hindu Pasts: Women, Religion, Histories</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SUNY_Press" title="SUNY Press">SUNY Press</a>. p. 310. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781438468075" title="Special:BookSources/9781438468075"><bdi>9781438468075</bdi></a>. <q>On the issue of vocabulary, Ahmad goes on to cite Syed Ahmad Dehlavi as he set about to compile the Farhang-e-Asafiya, an Urdu dictionary, in the late nineteenth century. Syed Ahmad 'had no desire to sunder Urdu's relationship with Farsi, as is evident from the title of his dictionary. He estimates that roughly 75 per cent of the total stock of 55.000 Urdu words that he compiled in his dictionary are derived from Sanskrit and Prakrit, and that the entire stock of the base words of the language, without exception, are from these sources' (2000: 112-13). As Ahmad points out, Syed Ahmad, as a member of Delhi's aristocratic elite, had a clear bias towards Persian and Arabic. His estimate of the percentage of Prakitic words in Urdu should therefore be considered more conservative than not. The actual proportion of Prakitic words in everyday language would clearly be much higher.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hindu+Pasts%3A+Women%2C+Religion%2C+Histories&amp;rft.pages=310&amp;rft.pub=SUNY+Press&amp;rft.date=2017-07-31&amp;rft.isbn=9781438468075&amp;rft.aulast=Dalmia&amp;rft.aufirst=Vasudha&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.censusindia.gov.in/2011Census/Language-2011/Statement-1.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Scheduled Languages in descending order of speaker's strength - 2011"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <a href="/wiki/Registrar_General_and_Census_Commissioner_of_India" title="Registrar General and Census Commissioner of India">Registrar General and Census Commissioner of India</a>. 29 June 2018.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Scheduled+Languages+in+descending+order+of+speaker%27s+strength+-+2011&amp;rft.pub=Registrar+General+and+Census+Commissioner+of+India&amp;rft.date=2018-06-29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.censusindia.gov.in%2F2011Census%2FLanguage-2011%2FStatement-1.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Gambhir1995-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gambhir1995_51-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGambhir1995">Gambhir, Vijay (1995). <i>The Teaching and Acquisition of South Asian Languages</i>. <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Press" title="University of Pennsylvania Press">University of Pennsylvania Press</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8122-3328-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8122-3328-5"><bdi>978-0-8122-3328-5</bdi></a>. <q>The position of Hindi–Urdu among the languages of the world is anomalous. The number of its proficient speakers, over three hundred million, places it in third of fourth place after Mandarin, English, and perhaps Spanish.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Teaching+and+Acquisition+of+South+Asian+Languages&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Pennsylvania+Press&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8122-3328-5&amp;rft.aulast=Gambhir&amp;rft.aufirst=Vijay&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Brill1993-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Brill1993_52-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>First Encyclopaedia of Islam: 1913-1936</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brill_Academic_Publishers" title="Brill Academic Publishers">Brill Academic Publishers</a>. 1993. p. 1024. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9789004097964" title="Special:BookSources/9789004097964"><bdi>9789004097964</bdi></a>. <q>Whilst the Muhammadan rulers of India spoke Persian, which enjoyed the prestige of being their court language, the common language of the country continued to be Hindi, derived through Prakrit from Sanskrit. On this dialect of the common people was grafted the Persian language, which brought a new language, Urdu, into existence. Sir George Grierson, in the Linguistic Survey of India, assigns no distinct place to Urdu, but treats it as an offshoot of Western Hindi.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=First+Encyclopaedia+of+Islam%3A+1913-1936&amp;rft.pages=1024&amp;rft.pub=Brill+Academic+Publishers&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=9789004097964&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKathleen_Kuiper2011">Kathleen Kuiper, ed. (2011). <i>The Culture of India</i>. <a href="/wiki/Rosen_Publishing" title="Rosen Publishing">Rosen Publishing</a>. p. 80. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781615301492" title="Special:BookSources/9781615301492"><bdi>9781615301492</bdi></a>. <q>Hindustani began to develop during the 13th century AD in and around the Indian cities of Dehli and Meerut in response to the increasing linguistic diversity that resulted from Muslim hegemony.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Culture+of+India&amp;rft.pages=80&amp;rft.pub=Rosen+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=9781615301492&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Prakāśaṃ-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Prakāśaṃ_54-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPrakāśaṃ2008">Prakāśaṃ, Vennelakaṇṭi (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=9WroLC__7EUC&amp;pg=PA185" rel="nofollow"><i>Encyclopaedia of the Linguistic Sciences: Issues and Theories</i></a>. Allied Publishers. p. 186. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9788184242799" title="Special:BookSources/9788184242799"><bdi>9788184242799</bdi></a>. <q>In Deccan the dialect developed and flourished independently. It is here that it receievd, among others, the name Dakkhni. The kings of many independent kingdoms such as Bahmani, Ādil Shahi and Qutb Shahi that came into being in Deccan patronized the dialect. It was elevated as the official language.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopaedia+of+the+Linguistic+Sciences%3A+Issues+and+Theories&amp;rft.pages=186&amp;rft.pub=Allied+Publishers&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=9788184242799&amp;rft.aulast=Prak%C4%81%C5%9Ba%E1%B9%83&amp;rft.aufirst=Vennelaka%E1%B9%87%E1%B9%ADi&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D9WroLC__7EUC%26pg%3DPA185&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-brown2008-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-brown2008_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFKeith_BrownSarah_Ogilvie2008">Keith Brown; Sarah Ogilvie (2008), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=F2SRqDzB50wC" rel="nofollow"><i>Concise Encyclopedia of Languages of the World</i></a>, Elsevier, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-08-087774-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-08-087774-7"><bdi>978-0-08-087774-7</bdi></a>, <q>Apabhramsha seemed to be in a state of transition from Middle Indo-Aryan to the New Indo-Aryan stage. <li id="cite_note-books.google.co.uk-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCoulmas2003"><a href="/wiki/Florian_Coulmas" title="Florian Coulmas">Coulmas, Florian</a> (2003). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kmKLxzTnL9IC&amp;pg=PA232" rel="nofollow"><i>Writing Systems: An Introduction to Their Linguistic Analysis</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-PeterDass2019-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-PeterDass2019_80-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-PeterDass2019_80-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPeter-Dass2019">Peter-Dass, Rakesh (2019). <i>Hindi Christian Literature in Contemporary India</i>. <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-00-070224-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-00-070224-8"><bdi>978-1-00-070224-8</bdi></a>. <q>Two forms of the same language, Nagarai Hindi and Persianized Hindi (Urdu) had identical grammar, shared common words and roots, and employed different scripts.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hindi+Christian+Literature+in+Contemporary+India&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2019&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-00-070224-8&amp;rft.aulast=Peter-Dass&amp;rft.aufirst=Rakesh&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-JainCardona2007-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-JainCardona2007_81-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JainCardona2007_81-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFJainCardona2007">Jain, Danesh; Cardona, George (2007). <i>The Indo-Aryan Languages</i>. Routledge. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-135-79711-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-135-79711-9"><bdi>978-1-135-79711-9</bdi></a>. <q>The primary sources of non-IA loans into MSH are Arabic, Persian, Portuguese, Turkic and English. Conversational registers of Hindi/Urdu (not to mentioned formal registers of Urdu) employ large numbers of Persian and Arabic loanwords, although in Sanskritized registers many of these words are replaced by <i>tatsama</i> forms from Sanskrit. The Persian and Arabic lexical elements in Hindi result from the effects of centuries of Islamic administrative rule over much of north India in the centuries before the establishment of British rule in India. Although it is conventional to differentiate among Persian and Arabic loan elements into Hindi/Urdu, in practice it is often difficult to separate these strands from one another. The Arabic (and also Turkic) lexemes borrowed into Hindi frequently were mediated through Persian, as a result of which a throrough intertwining of Persian and Arabic elements took place, as manifest by such phenomena as hybrid compounds and compound words. Moreover, although the dominant trajectory of lexical borrowing was from Arabic into Persian, and thence into Hindi/Urdu, examples can be found of words that in origin are actually Persian loanwords into both Arabic and Hindi/Urdu.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Indo-Aryan+Languages&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-135-79711-9&amp;rft.aulast=Jain&amp;rft.aufirst=Danesh&amp;rft.au=Cardona%2C+George&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
</li>

(10 January 2001). <a class="external text" href="https://dx.doi.org/10.1515/ijsl.2001.035" rel="nofollow">"The poisonous potency of script: Hindi and Urdu"</a>. <i>International Journal of the Sociology of Language</i>. <b>2001</b> (150). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1515%2Fijsl.2001.035" rel="nofollow">10.1515/ijsl.2001.035</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0165-2516" rel="nofollow">0165-2516</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=International+Journal+of+the+Sociology+of+Language&amp;rft.atitle=The+poisonous+potency+of+script%3A+Hindi+and+Urdu&amp;rft.volume=2001&amp;rft.issue=150&amp;rft.date=2001-01-10&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1515%2Fijsl.2001.035&amp;rft.issn=0165-2516&amp;rft.aulast=King&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+D.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdx.doi.org%2F10.1515%2Fijsl.2001.035&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHindustani+language"></span></span>
